Gaya","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya","contributor":{"__ref":"Contributor:kca://author/amzn1.gr.author.v1.msgio6b9mUlKluIgAWn9iA"},"id":27098959},"Review:kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.Z1AWmes2p_PTn7WcST9Gpg":{"__typename":"Review","id":"kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.Z1AWmes2p_PTn7WcST9Gpg","creator":{"__ref":"User:27098959"},"recommendFor":null,"updatedAt":1753678584422,"createdAt":1545146522708,"spoilerStatus":false,"lastRevisionAt":1624384969739,"text":"Mary Wollstonecraft, a teenager, was spending a vacation in Switzerland with her fiancé, \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/45882.Percy_Bysshe_Shelley\" title=\"Percy Bysshe Shelley\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ePercy Shelley\u003c/a\u003e, their mutual friend, \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron\" title=\"Lord Byron\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eLord Byron\u003c/a\u003e, and a few other people. Was the weather gloomy that summer of 1816? Were the companions bored to death? One evening, they challenged each other into writing the scariest ghost story they could come up with. No one remembers what the fellows wrote on that occasion. Except for the young woman’s literary creature and the misfortunes of Victor Frankenstein.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eSince then, what was meant as an entertaining story, rose to the dimension of a myth. So much so that the original novel itself has been covered up by layer upon layer of external imagery, which has very little to do with it — in particular, the heavily made-up face of Boris Karloff in the 1931 unfaithful film adaptation of this book. Nowadays, there are all sorts of adaptations (e.g. Kenneth Branagh’s movie, with De Niro, more on that below), parodies (Mel Brook’s \u003ci\u003eYoung Frankenstein\u003c/i\u003e), and probably even spooky porn versions.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eHowever, Mary Shelley’s novel is not so much about ghosts or monsters, as it is a meditation on the Biblical theme of Creation and Fall. Naturally, the idea of creating a living being — using some human technique instead of natural reproduction —, comes from the 16th-century Jewish narrative of the \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/99794.The_Golem\" title=\"The Golem by Gustav Meyrink\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eGolem\u003c/a\u003e of Prague. The subtle hints and overt references to Milton’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost\" title=\"Paradise Lost by John Milton\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eParadise Lost\u003c/a\u003e are also quite noticeable — the “daemon”, rejected from the start as an ugly duckling, learns to read with a copy of this book (?). At any rate, \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is a brilliant and existential reverie on the theme of God and Satan (Frankenstein and the “daemon”) and Adam and Eve (Frankenstein and Elizabeth / the monster and the potential lady-monster).\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eAnother striking aspect of \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e’s narrative is the Russian-dolls-like structure of the tales: first Captain Walton’s letters, which frame the whole novel, then Victor Frankenstein’s account and, finally, a tale within the tale, the \u003ci\u003edaemon\u003c/i\u003e’s story. This particular form is in keeping with earlier gothic novels and harks back to the \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/93101.The_Arabian_Nights\" title=\"The Arabian Nights by Anonymous\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eOne Thousand and One Nights\u003c/a\u003e, to which Mary Shelley might have had access, through Antoine Galland’s translation into French. Incidentally, Safie’s story, around the middle of the novel (another embedded tale within a tale), has some oriental undertones.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eMary Shelley’s book is considered one of the earliest examples of the Science Fiction genre. However, there is not much science or technology to speak of in \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, apart from a few mentions of \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/121121.Paracelsus\" title=\"Paracelsus\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eParacelsus\u003c/a\u003e and a couple of other alchemists and astrologers. The minor references to electricity, magnetism and galvanism are in the spirit of the times. Still, \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/112273.Michael_Faraday\" title=\"Michael Faraday\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eMichael Faraday\u003c/a\u003e, who would soon bring significant breakthroughs in these fields, was about the same age as the precocious author of \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e. Still, the presence of electromagnetism is not only a reference to the myth of Prometheus and the stolen fire. If anything, it expresses a fascination with landscapes: now sunny, beautiful and pleasant; now stormy, sublime and menacing, with ghastly thunderbolts ripping the clouds apart. Mary Shelley had a couple of predecessors — \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/11525.Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge\" title=\"Samuel Taylor Coleridge\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eColeridge\u003c/a\u003e is quoted a few times in her novel —, but that sort of imagery was, by and large, a novelty at the time. It might be interesting to note that while Mary Shelley was writing \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, Caspar David Friedrich was painting his famous \u003ci\u003eWanderer above the Sea of Fog\u003c/i\u003e (see below). This obsession with ominous landscapes would soon become a trope within the romantic and gothic literary tradition (cf. the often ridiculed \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/44512.Edward_Bulwer_Lytton\" title=\"Edward Bulwer-Lytton\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eBulwer-Lytton\u003c/a\u003e’s “dark and stormy night”).\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is also considered an early example of the modern Horror genre. However, the general impression is not exactly a feeling of terror. Rather a romantic and quite often bombastic expression of strong emotions: despair, anguish, despondency, melancholy, misery, wretchedness, affliction, etc., are words that come repeatedly under Mary Shelley’s pen. This accumulation of epithets might feel quaint and a little schmaltzy to a modern reader.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eEven so, \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e has probably been a significant inspiration to crime mystery novels, such as Stevenson’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/51497.The_Strange_Case_of_Dr__Jekyll_and_Mr__Hyde_and_Other_Tales_of_Terror\" title=\"The Strange Case of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde and Other Tales of Terror by Robert Louis Stevenson\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eDr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde\u003c/a\u003e and later avatars of serial killers on a murderous rampage. It has probably also exerted a strong influence on scary adventure stories, such as Poe’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/70925.The_Narrative_of_Arthur_Gordon_Pym_of_Nantucket_and_Related_Tales\" title=\"The Narrative of Arthur Gordon Pym of Nantucket and Related Tales by Edgar Allan Poe\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eNarrative of Arthur Gordon Pym\u003c/a\u003e, Wells’ \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/341272.The_Island_of_Doctor_Moreau\" title=\"The Island of Doctor Moreau by H.G. Wells\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eIsland of Doctor Moreau\u003c/a\u003e, or Lovecraft’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/32767.At_the_Mountains_of_Madness\" title=\"At the Mountains of Madness by H.P. Lovecraft\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eAt the Mountains of Madness\u003c/a\u003e. It might, nowadays, become once more a significant source of inspiration, as contemporary technology explores new forms of sentient and intelligent beings, out of GMO, silicon or some weird combination of the two.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1499445862i/23234250._SY540_.jpg\" alt=\"Caspar David Friedrich - Wanderer above the sea of fog\" class=\"gr-hostedUserImg\" loading=\"lazy\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_Shelley%27s_Frankenstein_(film)\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eMary Shelley’s Frankenstein\u003c/a\u003e (1994), directed by Kenneth Branagh, followed Francis Ford Coppola’s box-office hit \u003ci\u003eBram Stocker’s Dracula\u003c/i\u003e (1992). The cast is imposing: De Niro, Branagh, Hulce, Bonham Carter, Holm, Cleese. The screenplay (written by Frank Darabont, who would later develop \u003ci\u003eThe Walking Dead\u003c/i\u003e TV series) is, for the most part, faithful to Mary Shelley’s novel. However, while Coppola’s \u003ci\u003eDracula\u003c/i\u003e was darkly luxurious and decadent, the style of Branagh’s \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is loud and vehement, at times stomach-churning or downright silly.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThe biopic \u003ca href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_Shelley_(film)\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eMary Shelley\u003c/a\u003e (2017) by Haifaa al-Mansour, with Elle Fanning, is primarily a romance, recounting the complicated situation in which the young woman met her husband and how she got to write her masterpiece. The portrayals of Percy Shelley and Lord Byron are rather unflattering.","rating":4,"shelving":{"__typename":"Shelving","shelf":{"__typename":"Shelf","name":"read","displayName":"Read","editable":false,"default":true,"actionType":null,"sortOrder":null,"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/27098959?shelf=read"},"taggings":[{"__typename":"Tagging","tag":{"__typename":"Tag","name":"favorites","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/27098959?shelf=favorites"}}],"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/865164753"},"likeCount":579,"viewerHasLiked":null,"commentCount":57},"User:106644240":{"__typename":"User","legacyId":106644240,"imageUrlSquare":"https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1709490791i/106644240._UY200_CR0,0,200,200_.jpg","isAuthor":false,"viewerRelationshipStatus":null,"followersCount":0,"textReviewsCount":35,"name":"sara","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/106644240-sara","contributor":null,"id":106644240},"Review:kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.eODswowvEWweiDuJnc1RVQ":{"__typename":"Review","id":"kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.eODswowvEWweiDuJnc1RVQ","creator":{"__ref":"User:106644240"},"recommendFor":null,"updatedAt":1756304850752,"createdAt":1588726566909,"spoilerStatus":false,"lastRevisionAt":1588726724835,"text":"an annoying scientist and an ugly tall man fight over who is more depressed","rating":1,"shelving":{"__typename":"Shelving","shelf":{"__typename":"Shelf","name":"read","displayName":"Read","editable":false,"default":true,"actionType":null,"sortOrder":null,"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/106644240?shelf=read"},"taggings":[{"__typename":"Tagging","tag":{"__typename":"Tag","name":"romanticism-lit","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/106644240?shelf=romanticism-lit"}}],"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3080650658"},"likeCount":570,"viewerHasLiked":null,"commentCount":23},"User:15819476":{"__typename":"User","legacyId":15819476,"imageUrlSquare":"https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1641866972i/15819476._UY200_CR1,0,200,200_.jpg","isAuthor":false,"viewerRelationshipStatus":null,"followersCount":18581,"textReviewsCount":639,"name":"Bella","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/15819476-bella","contributor":null,"id":15819476},"Review:kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.a63bgVqithsZpQyejJQPnA":{"__typename":"Review","id":"kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.a63bgVqithsZpQyejJQPnA","creator":{"__ref":"User:15819476"},"recommendFor":null,"updatedAt":1756050242385,"createdAt":1601749279377,"spoilerStatus":false,"lastRevisionAt":1666287346917,"text":"\u003cb\u003e2nd read:\u003c/b\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003escientists just don't re-animate corpses like they used to it's disappointing\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cb\u003e1st read:\u003c/b\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eAll this time I thought I didn't like classics; turns out I just hadn't read the right ones. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eI can't help but feel empathy for Frankenstein's creature, and abhor humankind for its prejudice and malice that drove this creature to murder when all he craved was the warmth of companionship.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eI will never forgive modern media for making believe that this story was about a mindless, incoherent creature incapable of stringing two words together who's only destiny was to kill and be evil when in actuality all he wanted was FRIENDSHIP.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eI, too, relate to his absolute emo vibes; when he says \"Hateful day when i received life!\" and \"I was benevolent and good; misery made me a fiend. Make me happy, and I shall again be virtuous.\" I'm just like yeah exactly he gets it he knows","rating":5,"shelving":{"__typename":"Shelving","shelf":{"__typename":"Shelf","name":"read","displayName":"Read","editable":false,"default":true,"actionType":null,"sortOrder":null,"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/15819476?shelf=read"},"taggings":[{"__typename":"Tagging","tag":{"__typename":"Tag","name":"classics","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/15819476?shelf=classics"}},{"__typename":"Tagging","tag":{"__typename":"Tag","name":"gifted","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/15819476?shelf=gifted"}}],"webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3576890133"},"likeCount":495,"viewerHasLiked":null,"commentCount":4},"User:6431467":{"__typename":"User","legacyId":6431467,"imageUrlSquare":"https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1735525095i/6431467._UX200_CR0,11,200,200_.jpg","isAuthor":false,"viewerRelationshipStatus":null,"followersCount":13982,"textReviewsCount":1572,"name":"s.penkevich [mental health hiatus]","webUrl":"https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich-mental-health-hiatus","contributor":null,"id":6431467},"Review:kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.AnuHlNo4QbtHIO_InCpMyg":{"__typename":"Review","id":"kca://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.AnuHlNo4QbtHIO_InCpMyg","creator":{"__ref":"User:6431467"},"recommendFor":null,"updatedAt":1752861504126,"createdAt":1659658744361,"spoilerStatus":false,"lastRevisionAt":1729093382803,"text":"‘\u003ci\u003e’Man,’ I cried, ‘how ignorant art thou in thy pride of wisdom!’\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eFor over 200 years \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e by Mary Shelley has captured our minds and toyed with our fears, entering the canon of classic while remaining as relevant and thought provoking a metaphor as science progresses onward. It is certainly worthy of the lasting fame, being an exquisite blend of gothic horror and Romantic morality that delves into philosophical and allegorical inquiries in an endlessly engaging narrative that had me reading late into the night with a fervor to reach the end. It is a story we likely all know, and not much new to say someone hasn’t already said and better, but even still I was fascinated by every detail, with its fabulist monster story, the nested framing of two men on a quest in the arctic, the epistolary narrative, the plots of murderous vengeance and, of course, the fall from innocence with the damnation of ambition. This is also a story about how being a deadbeat dad passes along trauma that reacts negatively and is rather terrifying with its massive monster that moves fast and kills hard. This cautionary creation tale of catastrophe forces us to confront the grotesqueries of humanity grappling with life and death, and question what is truly monstrous.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eBeware; for I am fearless, and therefore powerful.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eThis is a book where the story about its creation is just as compelling as the novel itself. Written when Mary Shelley was 19, this would arrive on the scene under high expectations to see what the young writer of notable literary heritage (her father was \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/113910.William_Godwin\" title=\"William Godwin\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eWilliam Godwin\u003c/a\u003e and her late mother the feminist writer and activist \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/1853305.Mary_Wollstonecraft\" title=\"Mary Wollstonecraft\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eMary Wollstonecraft\u003c/a\u003e) would produce. ‘\u003ci\u003eMy husband,\u003c/i\u003e’ she writes in an introduction, referring to \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/45882.Percy_Bysshe_Shelley\" title=\"Percy Bysshe Shelley\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ePercy Bysshe Shelley\u003c/a\u003e, ‘\u003ci\u003ewas from the first very anxious that I should prove myself worthy of my parentage and enroll myself on the page of fame.\u003c/i\u003e’ The novel sprang into creation supposedly during a dream (not unlike her doctor Frankenstein’s prophetic dream of creation) after \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron\" title=\"Lord Byron\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eLord Byron\u003c/a\u003e proposed to his friends they each should write a ghost story while they were all \u003ca href=\"https://www.history.com/news/frankenstein-true-story-mary-shelley\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003estuck indoors on a retreat together\u003c/a\u003e at Lake Geneva. This same retreat would also birth the novel \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/472966.The_Vampyre\" title=\"The Vampyre by John William Polidori\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eThe Vampyre\u003c/a\u003e by \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/26932.John_William_Polidori\" title=\"John William Polidori\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eJohn William Polidori\u003c/a\u003e, who based his villain on Byron himself because \u003ca href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4888409762?type=review#rating_498720393\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eLord Byron was kind of insufferable\u003c/a\u003e. And a deadbeat dad, not unlike Victor Frankenstein. The novel itself can be read as a commentary on the experience of writing it, something she teases in her introduction about the creative experience of an artist and to which she writes that ‘\u003ci\u003eI bid my hideous progeny go forth and prosper.\u003c/i\u003e’ Unlike her doctor, there is a sense of care over her creation. Now, onward!\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eTo examine the causes of life we must first have recourse to death.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eThis book is sort of the antidote to those cliche inspirational office posters about ambition everyone had in the 90s, because here we find ambition to drip into arrogance and basically create calamity. Framed from the perspective of Cpt. Walton as he attempts to reach the North Pole—and fails—we hear the story of another man who’s blinded by his own ambition until it is too late. Doctor Victor Frankenstein’s story is a chilling fall-from-grace story, beginning with a sweet adolescence that slowly turns to the grotesque along a path of bloodied corpses of innocent people to chronicle his own loss of innocence. The spark to this is the early death of his mother, coupled with his reading of scientific books at university (self-education through books is mirrored as well through his monster), amalgamating into an idea with the best intentions to ‘\u003ci\u003erenew life where death had apparently devoted the body to corruption.\u003c/i\u003e’ We all know the maxim that good intentions are the paving stones to Hell, however. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eWhence, I often asked myself, did the principle of life proceed.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eWhile we tend to just remember the Doctor’s last name, the full title of the book is \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein; or, the Modern Prometheus,\u003c/i\u003e and here we begin to understand why fire and light is such a frequent symbol. As Prometheus gave fire to humans and was punished for it, we see Frankenstein attempt to ‘\u003ci\u003epour a torrent of light into our dark world\u003c/i\u003e’ by conquering death and creating life (with electric shock instead of fire but close enough), something that will be his own undoing. It is only natural, drawing on ideas of the Romantics that nature is the pinnacle of good and perfection and since he ‘\u003ci\u003ecollected bones from charnel houses; and disturbed, with profane fingers, the tremendous secrets of the human frame,\u003c/i\u003e’ to create and give life to his creation, therefore the manufactured (see also: “unnatural”) creation must also be profane and Frankenstein shall be punished. You know, the whole ‘don’t play god’ argument. The creature wrecks havoc on Frankenstein’s life as ‘\u003ci\u003ethe cold stars shone in mockery,\u003c/i\u003e’ and the barren Arctic is a sort of Hell (devoid of fire and warmth) where Victor inevitably meets his demise.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eYet Victor never views his ambitions as unnatural (him and the Captain being figuratively blinded by their ambitions functioning as another fire/light metaphor), and even at the end he says to Walton ‘\u003ci\u003eI have myself been blasted in these hopes, yet another may succeed.\u003c/i\u003e’ He sees no ethical dilemma in his manufacturing of life, his fears stem from the supposed hideousness of his creation—‘\u003ci\u003eugly…a thing such as Dante could not have conceived\u003c/i\u003e’—and then later the string of murders. ‘\u003ci\u003eA new species would bless me as its creator and source,\u003c/i\u003e’ Victor writes, propping himself up as a paternal godlike figure, ‘\u003ci\u003eno father could claim the gratitude of his child so completely as I should deserve theirs,\u003c/i\u003e’ however he fails as a father to his creation by abandoning it. He does not even give the creation a name, even when hunting it after the multiple murders. This is key, as in fairy tales naming something is a way to take away it’s power (think Rumpelstiltskin being defeated by guessing his name). ‘\u003ci\u003eI think of the act of naming as diagnosis,\u003c/i\u003e’ \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/15811.Rebecca_Solnit\" title=\"Rebecca Solnit\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eRebecca Solnit\u003c/a\u003e writes in her essay \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/39688744.Call_Them_by_Their_True_Names_American_Crises__and_Essays_\" title=\"Call Them by Their True Names American Crises (and Essays) by Rebecca Solnit\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eCall Them by Their True Names\u003c/a\u003e, ‘\u003ci\u003eonce you name a disorder, you may be able to connect to the community afflicted with it, or build one.\u003c/i\u003e’ Seeing as the creation turns to evil deeds due to a feeling of isolation and being othered, this idea of naming, of giving a space, makes the lack of naming or any parental care more emphasized as a fatal misstep. So not only does Victor have his fall from grace for trying to play god, but also failing in even the most basics of paternal support. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eAm i to be thought the only criminal, when all human kind sinned against me?\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eLet's turn now to Frankenstein’s monster. Left to his own devices, this giant manufactured from death finds he is met with fear and misunderstanding at every turn. While he seems to only have pure intentions at first, he is pushed into solitude and begins to lash out, especially at his creator (definitely some religious symbolism there). While he may be manmade, he is also very human all the way down to emotions and existential crises of selfhood:\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cblockquote\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eI was dependent on none and related to none. The path of my departure was free, and there was none to lament my annihilation. My person was hideous and my stature gigantic. What did this mean? Who was I? What was I? Whence did I come? What was my destination? These questions continually recurred, but I was unable to solve them.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e The creature conducts his autodidactic education mostly through reading and watching a family interact. Among his books are Goethe’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/16640.The_Sorrows_of_Young_Werther\" title=\"The Sorrows of Young Werther by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eThe Sorrows of Young Werther\u003c/a\u003e, and Milton’s \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/book/show/15997.Paradise_Lost\" title=\"Paradise Lost by John Milton\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eParadise Lost\u003c/a\u003e, the latter being very influential on his impressions of the world and themself. From Milton he reads himself first as Adam in the creation story, but later identifies with Satan instead:\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cblockquote\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eI am thy creature: I ought to be thy Adam; but I am rather the fallen angel, whom thou drivest from joy for no misdeed. Everywhere I see bliss, from which I alone am irrevocably excluded. I was benevolent and good; misery made me a fiend. Make me happy, and I shall again be virtuous.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eThough he also notes that ‘\u003ci\u003eyet even that enemy of God and man had friends and associates in his desolation; I am alone\u003c/i\u003e’ Milton pops up everywhere in his sections, such as the monsters statement that ‘\u003ci\u003eevil thenceforth became my good,\u003c/i\u003e’ which paraphrases Milton’s line ‘\u003ci\u003eEvil be thou my good.\u003c/i\u003e’ In an \u003ca href=\"http://knarf.english.upenn.edu/Articles/oates.html\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eessay on the novel\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/3524.Joyce_Carol_Oates\" title=\"Joyce Carol Oates\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eJoyce Carol Oates\u003c/a\u003e argues that the monster’s surprise at his reflection in the water is not a reference to Narcissus as is typically claimed, but instead a reference to Eve from Paradise Lost: ‘\u003ci\u003eOf sympathy and love; there I had fixt / Mine eyes till now, and pin’d with vain desire.\u003c/i\u003e’ This, she argues, makes the monster a sort of reverse holy trinity of creation instead of creator, speaking from Milton’s Adam, Eve and Satan as opposed to the Father, Son and Holy Ghost. There is another interesting reversal that Shelley plays with, something \u003ca href=\"http://knarf.english.upenn.edu/Articles/veeder.html\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eWilliam Veeder calls\u003c/a\u003e the ‘negative Oedipus.’ The monster kills Elizabeth to get to his ‘father’, Frankenstein, while the death of the doctor’s mother is his motivation to play Holy Father. At all times Shelley constructs a duality of parenthood and horror.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eWhile the classic human vs nature and the whole ethical conundrum is fascinating, it is the look at the person under the extreme absence of love and support that grabs me most. On one hand it is an intriguing look at the horrors of isolation and being othered, but it also makes me wonder how narratives like this became concrete in collective consciousness and instill further fear into people against anything different. Is this also central to ideas of xenophobia and pushbacks against equality, the fear that if we allow the marginalized to have space they will harm the people-of-status-quo/colonizers as a state of revenge? Thinking about it that way is frustrating and sad, the idea that those who have been harmed continue to be so out of fear for the repercussions, which only furthers the othering and marginalizing. So I guess that’s something to consider too and work on undoing in social consciousness. And a reminder to give love and empathy. The creation only wanted to be understood, given empathy and space, given love. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e‘\u003ci\u003eI could not understand why men who knew all about good and evil could hate and kill each other.\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eNow the question here proposed by Shelley is, who is the ‘true’ monster? The man who reached for the profane and abandoned it into a life of torment turning toward evil, or the misunderstood being thrust into the world already considered an abomination and becoming ‘\u003ci\u003emalicious because I am miserable.\u003c/i\u003e’ Its ethical quandaries like this that make this a fantastic classroom choice or one to toss and turn with for days. The National Theater had an excellent \u003ca href=\"https://www.nationaltheatre.org.uk/shows/nt-at-home-frankenstein#production-story\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003estage adaptation\u003c/a\u003e where the two leads, Benedict Cumberbatch and Jonny Lee Miller, alternated roles as Frankenstein and Frankenstein’s monster to further interrogate this question. Though perhaps the creation says it best: ‘\u003ci\u003eListen to me, Frankenstein. You accuse me of murder; and yet you would, with a satisfied conscience, destroy your own creature. Oh, praise the eternal justice of man!\u003c/i\u003e’\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eIn an \u003ca href=\"https://lithub.com/jeanette-winterson-and-mark-oconnell-on-the-future-of-humanity-in-a-tech-dominated-world/\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003einterview with LitHub\u003c/a\u003e, author \u003ca href=\"https://goodreads.com/author/show/9399.Jeanette_Winterson\" title=\"Jeanette Winterson\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eJeanette Winterson\u003c/a\u003e says of \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e that ‘\u003ci\u003ewe are the first generations to read it not as gothic horror but as contemporary reality.\u003c/i\u003e’ Where Shelley’s doctor steals body parts to create life with electricity, in our modern day we are pushing closer towards digital AI and already have manufactured body parts to replace our own. Even just this week it has been in the news that scientists \u003ca href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2022/08/03/science/pigs-organs-death.html\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003ehave revived cells in dead pig’s brains and other organs\u003c/a\u003e, something that could be a breakthrough in increasing viable organ transplants. Perhaps there is something to learn from Victor’s failures as we consider how to usher new systems into the world, such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.vox.com/recode/2020/2/18/21121286/algorithms-bias-discrimination-facial-recognition-transparency\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003eissues of bias in algorithms\u003c/a\u003e, or if an AI were to learn from us, what are we reflecting back to ourselves. Perhaps this is why Mary Shelley’s classic has endured all this time; with each new advancement in science many fear a Frankenstein unleashing his monster and with each ambition of our own we fear what may happen if we are blinded by our desires. Mary Shelley captures this perfectly and I was surprised how engaging this book was and how relevant it still felt all these years later.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr revived. se ha hablado de \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e. Se interpreta como una crítica al desarrollo científico, cuando este sobrepasa el curso natural de las cosas; se interpreta como una crítica a la religión y nuestra relación con Dios; hasta se ha dicho que es una alegoría a los miedos que surgen durante un embarazo. Todas estas lecturas probablemente sean correctas, pero omiten lo más básico. Lo que hace a \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e una obra atiborrada de humanidad, con interpolaciones que abordan la desventura a través de la complejidad de tres voces que derraman soledad y melancolía. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eSi intentara tratar a \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e desde lo más superficial, acabaría hablando sobre la profundidad del espíritu humano y los peligros que conlleva un uso desmedido de sus facultades cognitivas o la influencia del sentimiento en sus actos. No se puede discutir este título sin penetrar en lo más reflexivo de nuestra naturaleza. \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, en su perfil más somero, abarca temas que atraviesan la columna vertebral de lo que nos hace seres en constante conflicto de moralidades y en una interminable búsqueda por pertenecer y ser reconocidos, como asimismo incluye el derecho a ser amados por al menos alguien en la vida.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eA partir de un análisis ligero, \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e trata la venganza y el abandono. Son las dos cuestiones principales que hacen esta historia una historia con movimiento. La venganza desde el arrebato y la venganza desde el rechazo. También cabe destacar que es admirable la caracterización del Creador y su Criatura, y sus interminables pugnas por quién posee la más dañina amenaza; ya que, si lo vemos de esa forma, y teniendo en cuenta la completa implicación de las circunstancias en la novela, ¿qué es más nocivo, el rechazo injustificado o el asesinato como consecuencia de una iniquidad anterior?\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eLa denominada Criatura, Demonio, Engendro o Monstruo es de los personajes más humanos con los que me he topado. Es suntuosa la carga emotiva, el abigarramiento en el interior de un ser que fue engendrado por ciencia mal utilizada. Un vínculo irónico entre un hombre que quería ser dios y un ser que siempre quiso ser hombre. Para mí esta es la crítica más punzante de Mary Shelley. Repudia que nos dejemos llevar por las apariencias en vez de detenernos a pensar en lo que estamos haciendo. La egolatría, la soberbia y el prejuicio son lo más lamentable de nuestra conducta como humanidad, y esas condiciones danzaron alrededor del llamado Monstruo y acabaron destruyendo la bondad más pura, que, prácticamente, ni había llegado a florecer.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eNo voy a omitir la prosa, que acompaña a la perfección la calidad de este libro. No hay mejor pluma que la que te abraza y te lleva consigo en un viaje por el tiempo y el espacio hacia su historia. Mientras leía \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, me sentía allí, junto a Víctor y a su Criatura, percibía la realidad que me rodeaba como falsa, como un pobre bosquejo de lo que estaba leyendo, y creía que mi pertenencia radicaba en el siglo XVII. La ambientación de Shelley es tan estupenda que fue capaz de sobrepasar los límites de la narrativa e invadir el mundo real. Excelente trabajo de la autora. Su estilo de escritura es de los puntos más fuertes del texto.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e se ha transformado en una de mis obras favoritas. Su retrato de la soledad, el aislamiento forzado, la pérdida y la derrota es sublime. Un relato que se construye sobre lo emocional y que tiene sus raíces en lo más hondo del alma humana. The Tambora volcano eruption in 1815 completely changed Europe's climate, particularly between 1816 and 1817. It led to almost glacial periods in the Alps. Around this time, friends Shelley and Byron traveled around Switzerland on vacation. The lousy weather obliges them to often remain secluded in their chalet, with, like a favorite pastime, to invent extraordinary stories. During one of these evenings, it would be born, from the imagination of Mary Shelley, the character of Frankenstein and his creature - feeding on romantic, gothic, and fantastic literature, with the scientific spirit in vogue at the time of the early 19th century. loading=\"lazy\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003ci\u003e(Full-size image \u003ca href=\"http://harkavagrant.com/index.php?id=56\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\"\u003ehere\u003c/a\u003e.)\u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eMary was – what? – eighteen years old when she went on this famous holiday to Lake Geneva with Percy Bysshe Shelley and Byron and Byron's physician. She was calling herself ‘Mrs Shelley’, though they had not yet married – Percy was still married to someone else.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThe surroundings were familiar. The last time Mary and Percy had come to Switzerland had been during their elopement a couple of years earlier, accompanied by her sister, who was also in love with him; Mary had got pregnant, but the baby girl was born prematurely and died in February 1815. Now they were back, trying to put the past behind them and enjoy a holiday with Byron, who at the time was sleeping with Mary's stepsister. Percy's first wife would soon be out of the picture, found drowned in the Serpentine in an ‘advanced state of pregnancy’ before the year was out. Mary's other sister Fanny also drowned herself that year, 1816, also pining for Percy.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eSo it was in the midst of this complex love-dodecahedron that the holidaymakers, their festive plans foiled by constant rain, held their famous competition to write a ghost story. The result is something very different from its image in popular culture. Instead of the smoke of Victorian London, we have the Swiss Alps and the Orkney Islands; instead of Igor and bolts through the neck, we have meditations on personal autonomy, scientific responsibility and eugenics.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is overwritten and the narrative structure is a bit odd – she was still a teenager when she wrote it, let's not forget – but thematically, it's fascinating. I'm surprised by how few reviews I've read touch on what seems to me to be the intensely female experiences that it obliquely comments on. The confusion of bringing a creature into the world only to feel horror and revulsion towards it. The stress of releasing it into a hostile and uncaring world. And perhaps most of all, the deep sympathy shown with someone who feels that their body is not their own, that it is somehow owned and regulated by others. A body that one is taught by society to hate. The monster's feelings are unimportant, because he was created by a man for the man's own gratification.\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eMary quotes her beloved Percy Bysshe Shelley, unattributively, when Dr Frankenstein first spots his creature up on the Mer de Glace. She uses the final two stanzas from ‘Mutability’. For me though it's the beautiful first stanza that better expresses the ferocious intensity of Mary and her circle of friends and lovers, surrounded as they all seemed to be by imminent, premature death:\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cblockquote\u003eWe are as clouds that veil the midnight moon;\u003cbr\u003e    How restlessly they speed, and gleam, and quiver,\u003cbr\u003eStreaking the darkness radiantly!—yet soon\u003cbr\u003e    Night closes round, and they are lost forever…\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eAs they all were. But the writing they left behind will last as long as English literature is read, and for all of its problems \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is among that select a://review:goodreads/amzn1.gr.review:goodreads.v1.WospIdgLwmyUtj-gKgpvyQ","creator":{"__ref":"User:1232712"},"recommendFor":null,"updatedAt":1754413889761,"createdAt":1603317483724,"spoilerStatus":false,"lastRevisionAt":1695419449272,"text":"\u003cb\u003e“It was on a dreary night of November that I beheld the accomplishment of my toils. With an anxiety that almost amounted to agony, I collected the instruments of life around me, that I might infuse a spark of being into the lifeless thing that lay at my feet. It was already one in the morning; the rain pattered dismally against the panes, and my candle was nearly burnt out, when, by the glimmer of the half-extinguished light, I saw the dull yellow eye of the creature open; it breathed hard, and a convulsive motion agitated its limbs…”\u003c/b\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e-\tMary Shelley, \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is a marvelous example of a book that escaped from its author and took on an entirely new existence beyond the boundaries of the page. There is an elegant symmetry to this. After all, the novel concerns a “monster” or “daemon” that slips away from its creator, the scientist Victor Frankenstein, to cause havoc in the world. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eToday, when we think of Frankenstein – or more accurately, about scientist Victor Frankenstein’s never-named creature – we are likely imagining something other than the somewhat vaguely-described human-ish thing in Mary Shelley’s 1818 horror classic. Unmoored from its literary roots, the Frankenstein’s monster we call forth in our minds is probably closer in conception to Boris Karloff’s famous version from the 1930s: square-headed and scarred, with metal bolts in the neck. This is a visage that is ubiquitous every October, found in movies, on television, and selling Halloween candy. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003ePart of the fun of reading \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is to see how far this cobbled-together man has traveled from Shelley’s original iteration. Frankly, this was probably the \u003ci\u003eonly\u003c/i\u003e fun I had, as \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, for all its bursts of creativity, its sudden flashes of discrete violence, is a bit of a wordy drag. It is fully a piece from the Romantic era, from the overwrought emotional excesses of its characters, to the gorgeous, travelogue-like descriptions of the Alps. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e***\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eAccording to Shelley – in an 1831 forward to the revised text – \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e had its genesis in a spontaneous parlor game between famed wordsmiths, of which she took part. Only eighteen at the time, Shelley was vacationing in Lake Geneva with her husband, the poet Percy Bysshe Shelley, and the famous Lord Byron. Byron apparently suggested they each write a ghost story, since the weather was too lousy to do much else. Mary Shelley’s contribution, according to this lore, was \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein; or, The Modern Prometheus\u003c/i\u003e. The short story she began in a cold and dispiriting Switzerland eventually became a full-length novel that is currently enjoying a two-hundred year afterlife. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e***\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eAs an aside, Shelley’s life, and her relationship with the doomed Percy, is worth exploring. There is free love, unfounded suggestions that Percy penned \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e, and tragedy aplenty.\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e*** \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e utilizes an epistolary framing device, both beginning and ending with a series of “letters” from an adventurous mariner to his sister. Initially, I was entirely uncertain what these missives had to do with Frankenstein and his monster. As a result, the novel started extremely slow for me, as I found myself reading only a couple pages at a time before losing interest, never really finding the hook. Ultimately – as you can see – I pushed through, but it was only much later, when the letters reappear at the end, that everything clicked into place (and I went back and reread the opening gambit). \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eOnce this prelude is out of the way, we begin the main part of the narrative, which is told in the first-person by the emotionally labile Frankenstein. For reasons put down to obsession, young Frankenstein is preoccupied with creating new life. Working alone and in fanatical devotion to his goal, Frankenstein begins assembling his \u003ci\u003ething\u003c/i\u003e: \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cblockquote\u003eI collected bones from charnel-houses; and disturbed, with profane fingers, the tremendous secrets of the human frame. In a solitary chamber, or rather cell, at the top of the house, and separated from all the other apartments by a gallery and staircase, I kept my workshop of filthy creation: my eye-balls were starting from their sockets in attending to the details of my employment. The dissecting room and the slaughterhouse furnished many of my materials; and often did my human nature turn with loathing from my occupation, whilst, still urged on by an eagerness which perpetually increased, I brought my work near to a conclusion.\u003c/blockquote\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eIt does not – \u003ci\u003eshould\u003c/i\u003e not – spoil anything to say that Frankenstein is successful in his endeavors, at least up to a point. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e***\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eThe monster or creature or daemon or whatever you’d like to call it – just don’t call it Frankenstein – comes to life and immediately begins to torment Victor. Unlike the shambling, slow-moving, slow-witted monster of modern pop culture, Shelley’s creature moves faster than the zombies in \u003ci\u003e28 Days Later\u003c/i\u003e, climbs mountains like Sly Stallone in \u003ci\u003eCliffhanger\u003c/i\u003e, and is an exceptionally advanced autodidact, not only teaching himself to speak, but to read \u003ci\u003eParadise Lost\u003c/i\u003e and \u003ci\u003ePlutarch’s Lives\u003c/i\u003e. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eFrankenstein and his monster engage in a globetrotting game of cat-and-mouse, which might have been exciting, if not written in the overly formal, cluttered style of the early nineteenth century. Shelley tells this story broadly, seldom taking any time to build a scene, ratchet up suspense, or pay off a plot arc. Instead, through the excitable, often operatically frantic voice of Frankenstein, we are given broad characters (Elizabeth, for instance, seems to be a forerunner of Dickens’s saintly – and boring – Esther Summerson), unexciting action, and pointedly telegraphed plot points. It says something that the high point of \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e is a story-within-a-story (in true Conradian fashion) told by the monster itself. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eHonestly, I have a predisposition against books written in the archaic-feeling style of \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e. It is something I always thought I’d grow out of as I aged. Alas, it has been two decades since high school, and my bias against writers who use \u003ci\u003ethee\u003c/i\u003e and \u003ci\u003ethy\u003c/i\u003e still abides. \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eThat is not to say I recommend skipping this novel, because I don’t. I also did not hate it, not by any means. The story works better in summary than in execution, and it requires close attention, but it is a genuine triumph of imagination. It is also far more polished and thematically coherent than Bram Stoker’s later attempt at Gothic horror (which has also turned out to be undying). \u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e***\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eCertain books have meanings beyond the composition of sentences and the contours of a storyline. Shelley created something in \u003ci\u003eFrankenstein\u003c/i\u003e that has endured for a couple centuries, and will likely live on forever, as long as people read. For that reason – if not \u003ci\u003eonly\u003c/i\u003e for that reason – this classic example of a thinking person’s horror novel is worth checking Rompo mi promesa. Jamás volveré a crear otro ser como tú, con tu misma deformidad y tus maldades.\u003cbr /\u003e-Quise razonar contigo, pero has demostrado que no quieres. Recuerda que soy yo quien tiene el poder. Te consideras desgraciado, pero piensa que sólo yo puedo hacerte tan desdichado que la luz del día te resultará odiosa. Tú eres mi creador, pero yo soy tu dueño. ¡Obedece! Ten cuidado porque a nada temo, y eso me convierte en poderoso.”\u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eFue en abril, durante el verano del año 1816 en Villa Diodati, una localidad Ginebra que el más famoso y más romántico de los Románticos, el poeta inglés, Lord Byron, organizó un desafío literario para escribir el cuento más terrorífico que se les ocurriera, junto a su médico personal, John Polidori, el célebre poeta inglés Percy Bysshe Shelley y su amante, Mary Goodwin, quien más tarde se convertiría en su esposa y con nuevo apellido le daría chispa a la vida de la criatura más emblemática, conocida y arquetípica en la historia de la literatura: el monstruo creado por el científico Víctor Frankenstein.\u003cbr /\u003eLos relatos de Byron, “El entierro”, y de Percy Shelley “Los asesinos”, están inacabados y por ende, es imposible saber cuál podría haber sido su final.\u003cbr /\u003eEl mejor relato de todos es del de John Polidori, “El vampiro”, que es el disparador de lo que en la actualidad conoceríamos a través de Bram Stoker con “Drácula”, y esto se debe a que \"El vampiro\" anticipa a la inmortal novela de Stoker 56 años antes de su publicación.\u003cbr /\u003ePero sería Mary Shelley quien al final terminaría ganando y todo surge a partir de un extraño y profético sueño que tuvo en ese castillo, luego de leer entre todos distintos cuentos de los más afamados escritores alemanes, siendo E.T.A. Hoffmann el referente más importante.\u003cbr /\u003eA la mañana siguiente, cuando despertó les contó lo que soñó: \u003ci\u003e“Vi a un pálido estudiante de arte impías, de rodillas junto al ser que había ensamblado. Vi el horrendo fantasma de un hombre que estaba tendido, y luego, por obra de algún ingenio poderoso, manifestaba signos de vida y se agitaba con movimiento torpe y semivital…”\u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eLa escena nos lleva directamente al momento en el que Víctor Frankenstein, en esa tormentosa noche demencial, casi de la misma manera que en el cuento “El sueño” que la autora escribiera dos años antes, exclama que si criatura está viva: \u003ci\u003e\"Era ya la una de la noche. Una lluvia lúgubre golpeaba los cristales y la vela estaba a punto de apagarse cuando, iluminado por el resplandor de la luz casi consumida, vi que el ojo amarillento y mortecino de la criatura se abría; respiró con dificultad y agitó sus miembros con un movimiento convulso.\"\u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr /\u003eIncreíblemente y como si fuera una trágica maniobra del destino, ese encuentro tendría un final funesto: en 1821 John Polidori se suicidaría pero le sobrevivirá su inmortal cuento y Percy Bysshe Shelley moriría ahogado a orillas del lago Leman, dos meses después y de todo ese dolor surgiría esta obra inmortal llamada “Frankenstein, o el moderno Prometeo” \u003ci\u003eque Mary Shelley escribiría con tan sólo dieciocho años.”\u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr /\u003ePero esto no es todo. Existe un dato que no muchos conocen acerca de Mary Shelley: cuando el cuerpo de Percy Bysshe Shelley es llevado ante ella, pide que le saquen el corazón, dado que cuando Shelley es cremado su corazón, por razones completamente inexplicables, ¡no se quemó! \u003cbr /\u003eAnte esta señal, ella pidió que lo envolvieran en hojas de sus propios poemas, lo pusieran en un estuche y lo colocaran en su ataúd cuando ella misma falleciera y de esta manera, cuando esto sucedió, la enterraron junto con el corazón de su amado.\u003cbr /\u003eMás Romanticismo que eso, \u003ci\u003eimposible. \u003c/i\u003e\u003cbr /\u003ePoco queda por agregar acerca de Frankenstein que no se haya escrito ya a estas alturas.\u003cbr /\u003eLa novela es y será una de las más representativas del Romanticismo. Contiene muchos elementos de este movimiento: desde la dualidad Víctor/Monstruo, que en cierta manera es una forma de temática del doble, el sufrimiento del que sabe que va a perder (en ambos casos), el titanismo romántico claramente expuesto en la obra, el juego peligroso con la ciencia (El extraño Caso del Dr. Jekyll y Mr. Hyde de Robert Louis Stevenson es otro caso y posteriormente lo será también “La isla del doctor Moreau” de H.G. Wells ) y la referencia de Prometeo (de allí el subtítulo de la misma), ese semi dios condenado al que hace referencia, inmortalizarán esta obra.\u003cbr /\u003eLa obra posee tres narradores bien diferenciados. En primer lugar leemos las cartas del capitán Walton quien nos presenta la historia de cómo encuentra al doctor Víctor Frankenstein en medio del lugar más congelado del Polo Norte.\u003cbr /\u003ePosteriormente, todos los sucesos que Frankenstein le cuenta a Walton, desde cómo es su niñez hasta la creación del horrendo monstruo y lo que sucede después ante la negativa suya de crearle una compañera y por último es el turno de la criatura quien le cuenta a Frankenstein todas las vicisitudes que tuvo que pasar hasta que encuentra nuevamente a su creador.\u003cbr /\u003eCuando uno cree que es terrible lo que lee acerca de lo que sufre Víctor Frankenstein, se queda sin palabras al enterarse de las miserias e injusticias que la criatura debe sobrellevar para no sucumbir.\u003cbr /\u003eEs extremadamente bella y a la vez terrible la manera en que la autora nos acerca ambas historias, ya que logra conmovernos en ambos casos, a punto tal que no sabemos cómo lectores a quién apoyar.\u003cbr /\u003eEl encuentro entre ambos y lo que surge de ello es épico y no existe otro adjetivo para describir tanta perfección.\u003cbr /\u003eLos diálogos son poderosos, las escenas subyugantes y el argumento está construido sin fallas para todo se vaya ensamblando con el correr de la lectura.\u003cbr /\u003eLa novela es cerrada magistralmente cuando los tres, Walton, Frankenstein y la criatura se encuentran y le ponen fin a este drama tan intenso, tan único, tan descollante.\u003cbr /\u003eEs algo verdaderamente espeluznante tratar de entender cómo pudo Mary Shelley, esta adolescente de dieciocho años en el siglo XIX, crear algo tan inolvidable y terrible. \u003cbr /\u003eMás de un estudioso de las letras se lo sigue
